<div id="review_1424027954" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1424027954" />
    <a title="Alex" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3144945-alex"><img alt="Alex" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594856152p2/3144945.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1424027954?book_show_action=true">Oct 24, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex" href="/user/show/3144945-alex">Alex</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=2015">2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=novel-a-biography">novel-a-biography</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=hot-sex">hot-sex</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=2018">2018</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1424027954" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8418926169574810970">Fairy tales are mostly about fucking. You already knew that, but it wasn't really <i>explicitly</i> pointed out until Angela Carter, the towering misty cult mid-1900s author, flipped them on their backs. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/794157923' title='My review of American Gods' rel='nofollow noopener'>Neil Gaiman</a> <a href='https://www.telegraph.co.uk/culture/books/11243761/Neil-Gaiman-Disneys-Sleeping-Beauty.html' title='primary source' rel='nofollow noopener'>said</a> she pointed out, "'You see these fairy stories, these things that are sitting at the back of the nursery shelves?...Each one of them is a loaded gun.'" <br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1540945984i/26524352._SX540_.jpg' alt='angela-carter' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><i>Here she is looking like she's got a gun</i><br/><br/>The thing with fairy tales is that it's not just stories for children that are dark and lurid,</span>
  <span id="freeText8418926169574810970" style="display:none">Fairy tales are mostly about fucking. You already knew that, but it wasn't really <i>explicitly</i> pointed out until Angela Carter, the towering misty cult mid-1900s author, flipped them on their backs. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/794157923" title="My review of American Gods" rel="nofollow noopener">Neil Gaiman</a> <a href="https://www.telegraph.co.uk/culture/books/11243761/Neil-Gaiman-Disneys-Sleeping-Beauty.html" title="primary source" rel="nofollow noopener">said</a> she pointed out, "'You see these fairy stories, these things that are sitting at the back of the nursery shelves?...Each one of them is a loaded gun.'" <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1540945984i/26524352._SX540_.jpg" alt="angela-carter" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><i>Here she is looking like she's got a gun</i><br><br>The thing with fairy tales is that it's not just stories for children that are dark and lurid, it's childhood itself. The reason they're so enduringly popular with children is that they hint at the huge dark secrets, death and danger and sex, that we were very busy trying to deal with already. (The originals, of course, had all the violence but not the fucking; later versions were increasingly pasteurized.) You always knew <i>Little Red Riding Hood</i> was about fucking. The story didn't exactly say it and you didn't either, because if anyone said it out loud you wouldn't be allowed to read it. It was subtle enough so that you could think about it in your private head. But we weren't idiots, right? We knew all these things were there, and they seemed like they had a lot of shadows and maybe some sparkles, and we had a lot to think about.<br><br>Carter unpacked lots of things, not just fairy tales. Her biographer <a href="https://www.theguardian.com/books/2016/oct/01/angela-carter-far-from-fairytale-edmund-gordon" title="Guardian piece" rel="nofollow noopener">Edmund Gordon says:</a><br><blockquote>At a time when English literature was dominated by sober social realists, she played with disreputable genres – gothic horror, science fiction, fairytale – and gave free rein to the fantastic and the surreal. Her work is funny, sexy, frightening and brutal, and is always shaped by a keen, subversive intelligence and a style of luxuriant beauty. She was concerned with unpicking the mythic roles and structures that underwrite our existences – in particular the various myths of gender identity.</blockquote><br>But fairy tales are what she's about here, in her best-known book, and those myths of gender identity. The frisson we used to feel reading these stories was because we were always afraid (or hoping, or both) that a <i>girl</i> was about to be <i>defiled.</i> They were victims. Carter flips that, making the woman the lead actor. In <i>The Erl-King</i> this lady realizes that the wild man of the woods is going to turn her into a bird and put her in a cage as soon as they're done fucking, and what does she do? <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[She strangles him and sets all his other <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2148310454" title="My review of Anais Nin, who also flips the perception of women as victims" rel="nofollow noopener">little birds</a> free. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> You probably already know that the title novella, <i>The Bloody Chamber,</i> is based on Bluebeard. There will be ex-wives! <br> <br>During her lifetime no one was quite sure what to do with Carter, who was messing with magical realism and "<a href="https://electricliterature.com/oh-slippery-slipstream-who-is-the-weirdest-genre-of-them-all-755bead4389c" title="what is slipstream, you ask" rel="nofollow noopener">slipstream</a>" way before it was cool. Now she's the patron saint of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2234188654" title="My review of 2017's Her Body and Other Parties" rel="nofollow noopener">Carmen Maria Machado,</a> <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/66657.Magic_for_Beginners" title="I haven't read this yet - I'm not very good at reading short stories if we're being honest" rel="nofollow noopener">Kelly Link,</a><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1360176449" title="My review of Oranges Aren't The Only Fruit" rel="nofollow noopener">Jeanette Winterson,</a> <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/43763.Interview_with_the_Vampire" title="I really ought to re-read this" rel="nofollow noopener">Anne Rice,</a> about a million movies...I mean, and she's still obscure. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1540945984i/26524353._SX540_.jpg" alt="company-of-wolves" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><i>Company of Wolves, directed by Neil Jordan, based on the story from this book</i><br><br>To grow up and have a grown-up conversation about those stories sends this little thrill through us, then. In a way, it feels like we got busted! "They knew all along that these were dirty?!" And in another way, we realize that we're not as alone in our private heads as we maybe thought we were. In <i>The Company of Wolves,</i> Riding Hood comes on the wolf after he eats her grandmother and she's like, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[well, are we going to fuck or what? <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> In <i>The Tiger's Bride,</i> she finds out that her husband is a tiger and you're like oh shit, right? A tiger! And she's like <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[lol, I'm a tiger too, let's do it. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> We're all monsters up in here, friends. Let's get weird.<br><br><br><br>Here are my notes on the individual stories, with <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br><br><b>Bloody Chamber</b><br>Bony girl becomes fourth wife of Marquis who wears a real monocle. He’s the kind of dude who puts mirrors all over his bedroom. Carter certainly would like to work in a bunch of references to the Decadent movement, a 19th-century thing involving Gothicness and symbolism and dirty stuff. Oscar Wilde was involved, and Baudelaire. Here are some of the references I looked up:<br><br>- The novel <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/315298.L__Bas__Down_There_" title="Là-Bas (Down There) by Joris-Karl Huysmans" rel="nofollow noopener">Là-Bas</a><br>- <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eliphas_Levi" rel="nofollow noopener">Eliphas Levi</a> was a French magician<br>- The 1828 erotic novel <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1634719.The_Lustful_Turk_Or_Scenes_In_The_Harem_Of_An_Eastern_Potentate__Wordsworth_Classic_Erotica_" title="The Lustful Turk Or Scenes In The Harem Of An Eastern Potentate (Wordsworth Classic Erotica) by Anonymous" rel="nofollow noopener">The Lustful Turk: Or Scenes In The Harem Of An Eastern Potentate</a> features a lady who gets raped and is kindof into it. I haven't read it. It's said to be the inspiration for the made-up book "Adventures of Eulalie at the Harem of the Grant Turk."<br>- The French Symbolist painter Gustave Moreau never made a painting called "The Sacrificial Victim," but he did make <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gustave_Moreau#Gallery" rel="nofollow noopener">a number of other paintings of naked ladies.</a> Here's one. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1540945984i/26524351._SY540_.jpg" alt="sphinx" height="300" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><i>Is...is Oedipus going to fuck that sphinx? wtf bro</i><br><br>Puss in boots<br>Cat helps his master murder the rich old husband of a hot lady, after which they get married and live happily ever after.<br><br>Erl king<br>Atmospheric fairytale woods; wild man who seduces a virgin. She realizes he will turn her into a bird and keep her in a cage. Sudden shift from first to third person as she kills him and sets the birds free.<br><br>Snow child<br>Wife tried to murder her; count fucks her corpse. <br><br>Lady of the house of love<br>A young beautiful vampire who hates killing molders away in her rotting castle until a dashing young man stumbles into her lair. Is this the inspiration for Anne Rice, for Twilight? It has some of that adolescent romance. The vampire kills herself instead of eating him. He rejoins his company, soon to learn shuddering in the trenches of whatever world war.<br><br>Werewolf<br>Love this brief one! Flips little red riding hood. Girl maims a wolf that tries to eat her in the woods; arrives at grandmothers to find her maimed in the same way. Wild paw has turned into severed grandma hand. Villagers stone her to death and girl gets her house.<br><br>Company of wolves<br>Riding hood again, a werewolf again, he kills grandma but then she’s into it and they fuck instead of him eating her.<br><br>Wolf-Alice<br>Some sort of Nell girl is housekeeper for a vampire? I didn’t totally follow this one and should maybe read it again.<br> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="8418926169574810970" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2186314114?book_show_action=true">Nov 16, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1944168-m-a-nichols?shelf=retellings-fairy-tales">retellings-fairy-tales</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2186314114" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2084660329661937381">There are times when I read a book and wonder if I'm reading the same thing as the rest of the people who reviewed and loved it. This was definitely not was I was expecting, and definitely not worth reading.<br/><br/>First, the stories weren't particularly groundbreaking, unless you count adding a bunch of sex into fairy tales groundbreaking. For the most part, the stories stuck to the original source material, really only deviating to add sex or death or both. Not that either were particularly graphic, b</span>
  <span id="freeText2084660329661937381" style="display:none">There are times when I read a book and wonder if I'm reading the same thing as the rest of the people who reviewed and loved it. This was definitely not was I was expecting, and definitely not worth reading.<br /><br />First, the stories weren't particularly groundbreaking, unless you count adding a bunch of sex into fairy tales groundbreaking. For the most part, the stories stuck to the original source material, really only deviating to add sex or death or both. Not that either were particularly graphic, but just enough to be uncomfortable to those who don't like sexual content in their literature and not enough for those who do. I love dark stories and I love dark takes on fairy tales, but in these stories it felt gratuitous. Granted, I will admit I didn't read the whole book, but I got through half of it and found nothing unique. Unless you count Bluebeard being into BDSM and kink as being unique.<br /><br />On top of that, the whole point of retelling fairy tales is to put a new spin or give the stories new depth (otherwise, what's the point...just keep the originals), but these don't do either. Love at first sight abounds with no character dimension to back it up. The stories really felt like she just copied them out of the original source material and added a few more graphic details.<br /><br />And by the way, I have to mention the fact that so many of the reviews of this book rave about the feminist undertones of the stories, and I want to know how in the world anyone sees that in these stories. The female characters aren't any more "empowered" than the originals. They still fall in love with stupid men, allow themselves to be doormats, and are shallow, vain, and only seem to care about clothes and money. The only thing feminist about it is that it definitely paints men in a bad light. Every single one was either weak and pathetic or psychotic.<br /><br />Second, the writing is convoluted and nonsensical in some parts and trying too hard in others. One moment, the author uses a dozen different metaphors to describe a single piece of jewelry or an expression on their face, and the next she's skipping any descriptions of what's happening. Characters are sitting at a table in one sentence and the next they're in a completely different time and space with no explanation of how or why they got there.<br /><br />Frankly, it's a pointless collection of stories. They have enough of the original material to make them uninteresting and unoriginal and enough of the author's writing style to make them difficult to slog through at times. Don't bother.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2084660329661937381" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer17505424372544117004"><b>The wolfsong is the sound of the rending you will suffer, in itself a murdering.</b><br/><br/>As a rule I don't care for folklore. I also maintain a historical aversion to short stories. What a joy it is then to proclaim my love for these macabre tales of hymens, fogged mirrors, and the gasps of lusts and bloodletting. Ms. Carter's tales are fevered variations on nursery rhymes: Bluebeard, Red Riding Hood Lycanthropes and wee wicked Alice dart from the shadows and dazzle the reader.</span>

<div id="review_2954519244" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2954519244" />
    <a title="Teresa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/541416-teresa"><img alt="Teresa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1570841350p2/541416.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2954519244?book_show_action=true">Aug 27, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Teresa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Teresa" href="/user/show/541416-teresa">Teresa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143107613" href="/book/show/23281612-the-bloody-chamber-and-other-stories">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2954519244" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13009375605177829512">This book first came out in 1979, the year I graduated from high school. Not as controversial now as it was then, its relevancy is still in evidence. I enjoy retellings of traditional tales and am usually disappointed by them, so most of the stories were exciting to me.<br/><br/>The title story is a Bluebeard tale, but it’s also a re-working of <i>Beauty and the Beast</i>. Immediately following it are two obvious re-workings of the latter: the first, straightforward and rather faithful to the original; the secon</span>
  <span id="freeText13009375605177829512" style="display:none">This book first came out in 1979, the year I graduated from high school. Not as controversial now as it was then, its relevancy is still in evidence. I enjoy retellings of traditional tales and am usually disappointed by them, so most of the stories were exciting to me.<br /><br />The title story is a Bluebeard tale, but it’s also a re-working of <i>Beauty and the Beast</i>. Immediately following it are two obvious re-workings of the latter: the first, straightforward and rather faithful to the original; the second, brutal, blunt, and beautiful. Ending the collection are three tales of wolves—two are re-workings of <i>Little Red Riding Hood</i>, though no Woodsman is in sight in the first; and the third contains <i>Alice in Wonderland</i>-mirror echoes. <br /><br />Lush prose; phrases like “the bloody chamber” (Imagine all that can entail.); and flowery and bestial imagery (some of the latter, such as the licking used in several of the endings, perhaps a bit too repetitive) are woven throughout. Reversals of tradition are another trademark of the collection, including that of a vampire/<i>Sleeping Beauty</i> tale, as well as the ending of “The Erl-King,” though the latter holds more detailed description than narrative. <br /><br />I didn’t care for “Puss-in-Boots,” but even as a child I didn’t care for the original; plus ribald humor is not my thing. The sexual take on my favorite of the collection, “The Snow Child,” is the opposite of bawdy. I loved how quickly, symbolically, and honestly, the story unfolded.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13009375605177829512" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2166031671" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer14524031985188582022">I would never encourage anyone else to write like Angela Carter, because in almost any other hands it would be disastrous. But she somehow creates this feast of linguistic decadence that’s sensual, dark, dreamy, and sometimes laugh-out-loud funny. This short story collection was 160 pages of Carter seducing the English language, and the English language fell HARD. The 10 stories mostly feature fairytale retellings that are as haunting and atmospheric as they are analytical. Carter asks questions</span>
  <span id="freeText14524031985188582022" style="display:none">I would never encourage anyone else to write like Angela Carter, because in almost any other hands it would be disastrous. But she somehow creates this feast of linguistic decadence that’s sensual, dark, dreamy, and sometimes laugh-out-loud funny. This short story collection was 160 pages of Carter seducing the English language, and the English language fell HARD. The 10 stories mostly feature fairytale retellings that are as haunting and atmospheric as they are analytical. Carter asks questions about sexuality, evil, and human nature, and how all these themes are shaped by the very structure of traditional fairytales. Brilliantly subversive and luxuriously creepy. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14524031985188582022" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer6394839343223757621">Angela Carter is quite a wordsmith, and she certainly LOVES her commas. Her writing is mesmerizing to be sure. Reading her stories, however, reminded me of reading "Her Body and Other Parties." Some stories went over my head, some I liked, only one I really enjoyed. "The Bloody Chamber" is a must read, IMO. It's a fairly straight forward "Blue Beard" retelling, with a mood kind of like Du Maurier's "Rebecca," but also with an erotic bent. Could have done without the HEA, but oh well. The rest of</span>
  <span id="freeText6394839343223757621" style="display:none">Angela Carter is quite a wordsmith, and she certainly LOVES her commas. Her writing is mesmerizing to be sure. Reading her stories, however, reminded me of reading "Her Body and Other Parties." Some stories went over my head, some I liked, only one I really enjoyed. "The Bloody Chamber" is a must read, IMO. It's a fairly straight forward "Blue Beard" retelling, with a mood kind of like Du Maurier's "Rebecca," but also with an erotic bent. Could have done without the HEA, but oh well. The rest of the collection is odd - you have two versions of "Red Riding Hood" and two versions of "Beauty and the Beast," plus some werewolves and vampires. </span>

<div id="review_2534090015" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2534090015" />
    <a title="~Bookishly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28323108-bookishly"><img alt="~Bookishly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605967142p2/28323108.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2534090015?book_show_action=true">Jan 10, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="~Bookishly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="~Bookishly" href="/user/show/28323108-bookishly">~Bookishly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/18212039-the-bloody-chamber-and-other-stories">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28323108-bookishly?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28323108-bookishly?shelf=erotica">erotica</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2534090015" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8212810945294952874">Angela Carter's collection of short stories are certainly a different way of retelling the fairytales that we know and have grown to love. I enjoyed some of these stories, but some just went completely over my head. I particularly liked the first story "The bloody chamber" It was well written, intriguing, slightly erotic, and pretty haunting. It was probably my favourite, actually. The one I really disliked, was "The snow child" and it was only around five pages long. It certainly had the shock </span>
  <span id="freeText8212810945294952874" style="display:none">Angela Carter's collection of short stories are certainly a different way of retelling the fairytales that we know and have grown to love. I enjoyed some of these stories, but some just went completely over my head. I particularly liked the first story "The bloody chamber" It was well written, intriguing, slightly erotic, and pretty haunting. It was probably my favourite, actually. The one I really disliked, was "The snow child" and it was only around five pages long. It certainly had the shock factor, and just wasn't what I expected. <br />These stories are definitely creative, and are quite honestly weird, and that may be another reason why I enjoyed them. They didn't blow me away or anything, but it was a weird and wonderful way of retelling fairytales. I will be looking out for more from Carter.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8212810945294952874" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('bSZBFYyGwAdDUv16He+s+yueloGDtOc39Ef/5Gi7qw1XACjHN9gJb+R5SRvqGqYk/cFxSU05aVCvOblVpfhZlw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('gx++hPysN1Ehc3X44xvPX4VMXbR02NGH8U3+jCGIsPq5OddWR/L+OYZYwZkU7sWAUxO6fLpVX+CqM7g97MtCYA==')})
  }

In other words, a ghost story written by Jane Austen?\n\nAlas, we cannot give you Austen, but Susan Hill\'s remarkable Woman In Black comes as close as our era can provide. Set on the obligatory English moor, on an isolated causeway, the story has as its hero Arthur Kipps, an up-and-coming young solicitor who has come north from London to attend the funeral and settle the affairs of Mrs. Alice Drablow of Eel Marsh House. The routine formalities he anticipates give way to a tumble of events and secrets more sinister and terrifying than any nightmare: the rocking chair in the deserted nursery, the eerie sound of a pony and trap, a child\'s scream in the fog, and most dreadfully--and for Kipps most tragically--The Woman In Black.\n\nThe Woman In Black is both a brilliant exercise in atmosphere and controlled horror Henryson\'s finest poem, and one of the rhetorical masterp<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText5959414706096749818\" style=\"display:none\">The greatest of the late medieval Scots makars, Robert Henryson was influenced by their vision of the frailty and pathos of human life, and by the inherited poetic example of Geoffrey Chaucer. Henryson\'s finest poem, and one of the rhetorical masterpieces of Scots literature, is the narrative Testament of Cresseid. Set in the aftermath of the Trojan War, the Testament completes the story of Chaucer\'s Troilus and Criseyde, offering a tragic account of its faithless heroine\'s rejection by her lover, Diomede, and of her subsequent decline into prostitution and leprosy. Written in Middle Scots, the Testament has been translated by Seamus Heaney into a confident but faithful idiom that matches the original verse form and honors the poem\'s unique blend of detachment and compassion.\n\nA master of high narrative, Henryson was also a comic master of the verse fable, and his burlesques of human weakness in the guise of animal wisdom are delicately pointed with irony. Seven of the Fables are here sparklingly translated by Heaney, their freshness rendered to the last claw and feather. Together, The Testament of Cresseid and Seven Fables provide a rich and wide-ranging encounter between two poets across six centuries.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"5959414706096749818\" href=\"#\"       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/27500.Angela_Carter">About Angela Carter</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/27500.Angela_Carter"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1397683766p3/27500.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/27500.Angela_Carter">Angela Carter
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
2,611
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer7618760374565949609">Born Angela Olive Stalker in Eastbourne, in 1940, Carter was evacuated as a child to live in Yorkshire with her maternal grandmother. As a teenager she battled anorexia. She began work as a journalist on the Croydon Advertiser, following in the footsteps of her father. Carter attended the University of Bristol where she studied English literature.<br/><br/>She married twice, first in 1960 to Paul Carter. Th</span>
  <span id="freeText7618760374565949609" style="display:none">Born Angela Olive Stalker in Eastbourne, in 1940, Carter was evacuated as a child to live in Yorkshire with her maternal grandmother. As a teenager she battled anorexia. She began work as a journalist on the Croydon Advertiser, following in the footsteps of her father. Carter attended the University of Bristol where she studied English literature.<br /><br />She married twice, first in 1960 to Paul Carter. They divorced after twelve years. In 1969 Angela Carter used the proceeds of her Somerset Maugham Award to leave her husband and relocate for two years to Tokyo, Japan, where she claims in <i>Nothing Sacred</i> (1982) that she "learnt what it is to be a woman and became radicalised." She wrote about her experiences there in articles for New Society and a collection of short stories, <i>Fireworks: Nine Profane Pieces</i> (1974), and evidence of her experiences in Japan can also be seen in <i>The Infernal Desire Machines of Doctor Hoffman</i> (1972). She was there at the same time as Roland Barthes, who published his experiences in <i>Empire of Signs</i> (1970).<br /><br />She then explored the United States, Asia, and Europe, helped by her fluency in French and German. She spent much of the late 1970s and 1980s as a writer in residence at universities, including the University of Sheffield, Brown University, the University of Adelaide, and the University of East Anglia. In 1977 Carter married Mark Pearce, with whom she had one son.<br /><br />As well as being a prolific writer of fiction, Carter contributed many articles to The Guardian, The Independent and New Statesman, collected in <i>Shaking a Leg</i>. She adapted a number of her short stories for radio and wrote two original radio dramas on Richard Dadd and Ronald Firbank. Two of her fictions have been adapted for the silver screen: <i>The Company of Wolves</i> (1984) and <i>The Magic Toyshop</i> (1987). She was actively involved in both film adaptations, her screenplays are published in the collected dramatic writings, <i>The Curious Room</i>, together with her radio scripts, a libretto for an opera of Virginia Wolf's Orlando, an unproduced screenplay entitled <i>The Christchurch Murders</i> (based on the same true story as Peter Jackson's <i>Heavenly Creatures</i>) and other works. These neglected works, as well as her controversial television documentary, <i>The Holy Family Album</i>, are discussed in Charlotte Crofts' book, <i>Anagrams of Desire</i> (2003).<br /><br />At the time of her death, Carter was embarking on a sequel to Charlotte Brontë's Jane Eyre based on the later life of Jane's stepdaughter, Adèle Varens. However, only a synopsis survives.<br /><br />Her novel <i>Nights at the Circus</i> won the 1984 James Tait Black Memorial Prize for literature.<br /><br />Angela Carter died aged 51 in 1992 at her home in London after developing lung cancer. Her obituary published in The Observer said, "She was the opposite of parochial. Nothing, for her, was outside the pale: she wanted to know about everything and everyone, and every place and every word. She relished life and language hugely, and reveled in the diverse."<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7618760374565949609" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>

            <span class="readable">&ldquo;When I saw him look at me with lust, I dropped my eyes but, in glancing away from him, I caught sight of myself in the mirror. And I saw myself, suddenly, as he saw me, my pale face, the way the muscles in my neck stuck out like thin wire. I saw how much that cruel necklace became me. And, for the first time in my innocent and confined life, I sensed in myself a potentiality for corruption that took my breath away.&rdquo;</span>
